$(window).load(function(){

		success: function(data) {
			$(document).trigger('loginStatus', [data]);
			$(document).trigger('adminview',[data]);
		},
		error: function(xhr, textStatus){
			var data = { logged_in:null, error:textStatus };
			$(document).trigger('loginStatus', [data]);
		}
	});

	$('.commentsheading &gt; span &gt; a').click(function(){
		document.location.hash = 'comment';
	});
	}
});


$(document).ready(function(){
	$.ajax({
		url: "http://reason.com/ajax/commenthandle",
		dataType: 'json',
		success: function(data) {
			$(document).trigger('loginStatus', [data]);
			$(document).trigger('adminview',[data]);
		},
		error: function(xhr, textStatus){
			var data = { logged_in:null, error:textStatus };
			$(document).trigger('loginStatus', [data]);
		}
	});

	$('.commentsheading &gt; span &gt; a').click(function(){
		document.location.hash = 'comment';
	});

	if (window.location.hash.match(/^#comment/)) {
			var cctrl = $('.commentcontrol'), cctnr = $('#commentcontainer');
			cctrl.addClass('open');
			cctnr.show();
			$('#commentform.cancomment').show();
		
	}else{
			var cctrl = $('.commentcontrol'), cctnr = $('#commentcontainer');
			cctnr.hide();
			cctrl.removeClass('open');
			cctrl.one('click', function(e) {
				cctrl.addClass('open');
				cctnr.show();
				$('#commentform.cancomment').show();
			});
	}
	if($.cookie('fontSize')) {
		var fontSize = $.cookie('fontSize');
 		$('article.post').css({'font-size':fontSize});
 		$('ul.posts').css({'font-size':fontSize});
 	}

	// SIDR PANELS

	$('#mobile-nav-toggle').unbind('onclick');
	$('#mobile-nav-toggle').sidr({
		displace: false,
		name: 'sidr-main',
		source: '#site-navigation',
		side:'right'
	});
	jQuery('#sidr-main .sidr-class-closemenu a').click(function(){
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-main');
		jQuery('.small-share').show();
	});
	jQuery('.container_24').click(function(){
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-main');
	});
	jQuery('#mobile-nav-toggle').click(function(){
		jQuery('.small-share').hide();
	});


	$('#mobile-trending-toggle').unbind('onclick');
	jQuery('#mobile-trending-toggle').sidr({
		displace: false,
		name: 'sidr-trending',
		source: '#trending-navigation',
		side: 'left'
	});
	jQuery('#sidr-trending .sidr-class-closemenu a').click(function(){
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-trending');
        jQuery('.small-share').show();
	});
	jQuery('.container_24').click(function(){
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-trending');
	});
	jQuery('#mobile-trending-toggle').click(function(){
		jQuery('.small-share').hide();
	});
	jQuery(window).resize(function () {
		if (jQuery(window).width() &gt; 700) {
			jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-main');
			jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-trending');
		}
	});

	// let's do this in jquery so we're sure it's stacked
	$('textarea').focus( function() {
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-main');
		jQuery.sidr('close', 'sidr-trending');
	});
	// $.sidr('open', 'sidr-main');

	function setScaleable(x) {
		originalval = $('head meta[name=viewport]').attr('content');
		newval = originalval.replace(/(user-scalable=)\d{1,9}/g, '$1'+x);
		if (originalval.localeCompare(newval)) {
			$('head meta[name=viewport]').attr('content', newval);
		} else {
			$('head meta[name=viewport]').attr('content', originalval+', user-scalable='+x);
		}
	}

	//we don't want the screen to scale up when we select a text item, so we're going to prevent it by turning it on and off as we enter and leave
	$('textarea, input').on('focusin', function() {
		setScaleable(0);
	});

	$('textaream, input').on('focusout', function() {
		setScaleable(1);
	});

	// there's some visual element eh when people leave the screen, possibly because of fixed position
	// and element destruction order. We're going to simply hide these items on the way out to make sure
	// they don't jump around
	$( window ).unload(function() {
  		$('#nav1').hide();
  		$('#sidr-main').hide();
  		$('#sidr-trending').hide();
	});


	$("a#sidr-id-fontbigger").click(function() {
 		$('ul.posts').css('font-size', function(index,value) {
 			var fontSize = parseInt(value) + 1 + 'px';
	 		$.cookie('fontSize',fontSize, { path: '/', expires: 7 });
	 		return fontSize;
 		});
 		$('#right-col').css('font-size', function(index,value) {
 			var fontSize = parseInt(value) + 1 + 'px';
	 		$.cookie('fontSize',fontSize, { path: '/', expires: 7 });
	 		return fontSize;
 		});
  		$('article.post').css('font-size', function(index,value) {
 			var fontSize = parseInt(value) + 1 + 'px';
	 		$.cookie('fontSize',fontSize, { path: '/', expires: 7 });
	 		return fontSize;
 		});
	});

	$("a#sidr-id-fontsmaller").click(function() {
 		$('ul.posts').css('font-size', function(index,value) {
 			var fontSize = parseInt(value) - 1 + 'px';
	 		$.cookie('fontSize',fontSize, { path: '/', expires: 7 });

window.USERSTATE = {logged_in:null};

	$.extend(window.USERSTATE, d);

	$(document).bind('adminview', function(e, d) {
		if(d.isadmin){
			$("body").addClass("adminlogin");
			window.ADMININFO = JSONObject= {

		}
	});

function updateCountdowns() {
	editbuttons = $('button.comment_edit:visible');
	if (editbuttons.length) {
		editbuttons.each(function() {
			timeremaining = $(this).data('editwindow-remaining');
			if (--timeremaining &lt; 0) {
				$(this).text('edit timer expired');
				$(this).fadeOut(); //no need for further time update; we only poll visibles
			} else {
				$(this).text('edit ('+timeremaining+' seconds remaining)');
				$(this).data('editwindow-remaining', timeremaining);
			}
		})
	} else {
		//no reason to keep this running when there's nothing to update
		clearInterval(countdownTimer);
	}
}

var countdownTimer = null;

$(document).bind('loginStatus', function(e,d){
	servertime = Number(d.unixtime);
	editing_window_seconds = Number(d.editing_window_seconds);
	if (d.logged_in) {
		userID = d.user_id;
		var handle = $("#user_login_control")
		.html('&lt;p&gt;You are currently logged in as: &lt;strong&gt;&lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/manage" class="handle"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;. &lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/logout"&gt;&lt;span&gt;Sign Out&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;')
		.find('.handle')
		handle.text(d.handle)
		handle.attr('href', handle.attr('href')+'/'+userID);
		//look for all the editable comments by this user
		$('div#commentcontainer li[data-user-id='+userID+']').each(function( index ) {
			commenttimestamp = Number($(this).data('comment-timestamp'));
			elapsedtime = servertime - commenttimestamp;
			if (elapsedtime &lt; editing_window_seconds) {
				secondsremaining = editing_window_seconds - elapsedtime;
				$(this).find('button.comment_edit').show().data('editwindow-remaining', secondsremaining);
			}
		});
		countdownTimer = setInterval(updateCountdowns, 1E3);
	} else {
		$("#user_login_control").html('&lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/login"&gt;&lt;span&gt;Login&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/register"&gt;&lt;span&gt;Sign-up&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;');
	}
});

(function(){
	var threadedCommentRepliesActive=null;
	var cb = $('#commentcontainer');

	function cmtLoginStatusHandler(e,d) {
		var lc = $('#commentform'), clr = $('.comment_login_require');
		if (d.logged_in) {
			clr.hide();
			lc.addClass('cancomment');
			lc.find('.logged_in_as').remove();
			var handle = lc.children('form')
			.prepend('&lt;div class="logged_in_as"&gt;&lt;p&gt;You are currently logged in as &lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/manage/'+d.user_id+'" class="handle"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Not you? &lt;a href="http://reason.com/users/logout"&gt;Sign out&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;')
			.find('.handle');
			handle.text(d.handle);
			handle.attr('a', handle.attr('a')+'/'+d.user_id);
			lc.show();
			threadedCommentReplies(true);
			editComments(true);
		} else if (d.logged_in===false) {
			clr.show();
			lc.removeClass('cancomment');
			threadedCommentReplies(false);
		} else {
			clr.html('&lt;p&gt;Oops, something went wrong and we can\'t tell if you\'re logged in. Please referesh and try again.&lt;/p&gt;');
		}
	}

	function threadedCommentReplies(activate){
		var cb = $('#commentcontainer');
		if (activate) {
			cb.find('.comment_reply').show();
			cb.find('.comment_reply_msg').hide();
			if (!threadedCommentRepliesActive &amp;&amp; (typeof(CommentReplyForm) !== "undefined")) {
				var template = CommentReplyForm.preparetemplate('commentform');
				var crf = new CommentReplyForm(template);
				crf.bindHandlers('li.com-block button.comment_reply');
				var previewurl = $('#commentform form').attr('action').replace(/\/post$/, '/preview');
				CommentPreview.bindHandlers(template, template.find(':button.preview'), previewurl);
				CommentPreview.bindHandlers($('#commentform'), $('#commentform :button.preview'), previewurl);
			}
		} else {
			cb.find('.comment_reply').hide();
			cb.find('.comment_reply_msg').show();
			cb.find('.leave-comment').hide();
		}
	}

	function editComments(activate) {
		var cb = $('#commentcontainer');
		if (activate) {
			//we find only the ones made visible during initialization; no reason to bind the rest.
			// active_edit_buttons = $('button.comment_edit:visible');
			if (typeof(CommentEditForm) !== "undefined") {
				var template = CommentEditForm.preparetemplate('commenteditform');
				var crf = new CommentEditForm(template);
				crf.bindHandlers('button.comment_edit:visible');
			}
		} else {
			cb.find('button.comment_edit').hide();
		}
	}
	$(document).bind('loginStatus', cmtLoginStatusHandler);


})();

       <header class="mainheading">
        <hgroup>
         <h1 class="title">
          How a Federal Menu-Labeling Law Will Harm American Pizza
          <span class="editor" pid="177947">
          </span>
         </h1>
         <h2 class="subtitle">
          Complying with a proposed FDA menu-labeling rule would be somewhere between costly and impossible for tens of thousands of U.S. pizza and grocery chains.
         </h2>
        </hgroup>
        <p class="byline">
         <a href="http://reason.com/people/baylen-linnekin/all" rel="author">
          Baylen Linnekin
         </a>
         | January 5, 2013
        </p>
        <div class="options">

	});

									redirectUri: "http://reason.com/login_success.html"

								$(function () {
								    var spritzController = null;
									function spritz_177947() {
										function spritz_177947_visibility() {
											$('#spritzer_177947').toggle();
											$('#clicktospritz_177947').toggle();
										}
										function spritz_177947init() {
										    var onSpritzifySuccess = function(spritzText) {
												console.log('Spritzify success');
										        spritzController.startSpritzing(spritzText);
										    };
										    var onSpritzifyError = function(error) {
										        alert("Unable to Spritz: " + error.message);
										    };

											var customOptions = {
											        placeholderText:    { startText: 'Nope' },
											        header: 			{	login: false,
											        						close: true,
											        						closeHandler: spritz_177947_visibility },
													// redicleWidth:       300,
											        redicleHeight:      76      // Specify Redicle height
											};

											if (spritzController === null) {
												// Construct a SpritzController passing the customization options
												spritzController = new SPRITZ.spritzinc.SpritzerController(customOptions);
												// Attach the controller's container to this page's "spritzer" container
												spritzController.attach($('#spritzer_177947'));
										    }										    // this would be the most basic and inclusive way, but we really want to avoid reading photo captions etc
										    // text = $('#spritzer_177947').parents('article').find('div.postcontent').text();
										    var text = '';
										    var textNodes = $('#spritzer_177947').parents('article').find('div.postcontent').find('p');

										    // we want to exclude spans since they're how our image captions are enclosed
										    for (var i = 0; i &lt; textNodes.length; i++) {
												text += $(textNodes[i])
												    .clone()    //clone the element
												    .children() //select all the children
												    .remove('span')   //remove all the children
												    .end()  //again go back to selected element
												    .text() + '\n\n';
										        // text += $(textNodes[i]).text() + '\n\n';
										    }
											var locale = "en_us;";
										    // Send to SpritzEngine to translate
										    SpritzClient.spritzify(text, locale, onSpritzifySuccess, onSpritzifyError);
										}

										$(document).on('spritz_loaded', function() {
											console.log('Spritz loaded');
										   // Code that depends on a an initialized SpritzClient goes here
											spritz_177947_visibility();
											spritz_177947init();


										}); //end spritz_loaded

										if (typeof SpritzClient === 'undefined') {
											$.getScript('//sdk.spritzinc.com/js/1.2/js/spritz.min.js', function() {
												console.log('Spritz.min.js loaded');
											});
										} else {
											spritz_177947_visibility();
											spritz_177947init();
										}
									};
									$('#clicktospritz_177947').click(function() {spritz_177947(); return false;});
								})
            </script>
           </li>
           <li class="clear">
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </aside>
        <div class="clear">
        </div>
        <div class="audio" style="display:none;">
        </div>
       </header>
       <!--&lt;div class='google-ad'&gt;-->
       <!--&lt;/div&gt;-->
       <div class="p402_premium">
        <div class="entry postcontent">
         <p>
          This week, as a new Congress was being sworn in, the Food and
Drug Administration
          <a href="http://www.foodsafetynews.com/2013/01/fda-announces-two-long-awaited-food-safety-rules/#.UOcGT3PjkRs">
           released
          </a>
          two sets of controversial and long-delayed food-safety
rules.
         </p>
         <section class="asidecontainer p402_hide desktop-only">
          <aside class="relatedinfo">
           <section class="relatedarticles">
            <header>

              RELATED ARTICLES

              </p>
             </li>

                Geoffrey Zakarian: In Praise of Free Enterprise Food
               </a>
              </h4>
              <p class="meta">
               <a href="http://reason.com/people/nick-gillespie">

               </a>
               <span class="pipe">

               </a>

               <a href="http://reason.com/people/damon-w-root">

               </a>
               <span class="pipe">

           <section class="morearticles">
            <h3>
             MORE ARTICLES BY
             <a href="http://reason.com/people/baylen-linnekin/all">
              Baylen Linnekin
             </a>
            </h3>
            <ul>
             <li>
              <h4>
               <a href="http://reason.com/archives/2015/06/27/raisins-the-supreme-court-and-food-freed">
                Raisins, the Supreme Court, and Food Freedom
               </a>
              </h4>
              <p class="meta">
               <time datetime="2015-06-27T12:00:00+00:00">
                6.27.15 8:00 am
               </time>
              </p>
             </li>
             <li>
              <h4>
               <a href="http://reason.com/archives/2015/06/13/new-article-blasts-feds-pseudoscientific">
                New Article Blasts Feds Pseudoscientific Methods For Establishing Dietary Guidelines
               </a>
              </h4>
              <p class="meta">
               <time datetime="2015-06-13T12:00:00+00:00">
                6.13.15 8:00 am
               </time>
              </p>
             </li>
             <li>
              <h4>
               <a href="http://reason.com/archives/2015/05/30/dont-ban-trans-fats">
                Don't Ban Trans Fats
               </a>
              </h4>
              <p class="meta">
               <time datetime="2015-05-30T12:00:00+00:00">
                5.30.15 8:00 am
               </time>
              </p>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </section>
           <section class="relatedtopics">
            <ul>
             <li>
              <a href="http://reason.com/tags/food-policy">
               Food Policy
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <a href="http://reason.com/tags/regulation">
               Regulation
              </a>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </section>
          </aside>
         </section>
         <p>
          Another FDA rule thats been long in the making is the agencys
proposed menu-labeling rule.
         </p>
         <p>
          The purpose of that rule, first
          <a href="http://www.gpo.gov/fdsys/pkg/FR-2011-04-06/html/2011-7940.htm">
           proposed
          </a>
          in 2010 as part of the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act,
is to provid[e] information to assist consumers in maintaining
healthy dietary practices.
         </p>
         <p>
          In 2008, California became the first state in the nation to
adopt a uniform menu-labeling law. The law had the backing of the
states restaurant associationa surprising turn of events until
one considers that chain restaurants in the state had been forced
to deal with an increasing number of varying menu-labeling rules in
municipalities throughout the state. With more and more states
adopting their own menu-labeling rules, the National Restaurant
Association adopted the California strategy and sought a shield
against this death by 1,000 cuts by pushing for one uniform
national menu-labeling rule. (I detailed this chronology in a 2010
          <em>
           Chapman University Law Review
          </em>
          article,
          <a href="http://www.keepfoodlegal.org/PDFs/Linnekin.pdf">
           The California
Effect and the Future of American Food: How Californias Growing
Crackdown on Food &amp; Agriculture Harms the State &amp; the
Nation
          </a>
          .)
         </p>
         <p>
          The delay in implementing the FDAs menu-labeling rule appears
to have resulted, to the consternation of many in the food
industry, in an expansion of Congress original intent. As its now
constructed, the rule would apply not just to chain restaurants
like McDonalds and Applebees but also to grocery stores and chain
pizza restaurantsboth of which oppose the FDAs plans.
         </p>
         <p>
          Nancy Huehnergarth, executive director of the
          <a href="http://www.nyshepa.org/">
           New York State Healthy Eating and
Physical Activity Alliance
          </a>
          , tells me she supports the proposed
FDA rule because it would provide information at the right place
and time.
         </p>
         <div class="ym" id="ym_1044036704457308825">
         </div>
         <script type="text/javascript">
          (function(e,t){if(t._ym===void 0){t._ym="";var m=e.createElement("script");m.type="text/javascript",m.async=!0,m.src="//static.yieldmo.com/ym.m5.js",(e.getElementsByTagName("head")[0]||e.getElementsByTagName("body")[0]).appendChild(m)}else t._ym instanceof String||void 0===t._ym.chkPls||t._ym.chkPls()})(document,window);
         </script>
         <p>
          The key to menu labeling and getting it to work is that people
see the calories at the point of purchase, says Huehnergarth.
         </p>
         <p>
          Pizza chains, which have banded together to oppose the FDAs
plans to have the rules cover them, have good reasons to chafe at
being included under the law.
         </p>
         <p>
          For one, most have been providing nutrition information for
years. The Papa Johns
          <a href="http://order.papajohns.com/nutrition.html">
           website
          </a>
          displays
nutrition information under each menu item, for example, while
Dominos website features a tool it calls a
          <a href="https://order.dominos.com/en/pages/content/nutritional/cal-o-meter.jsp">
           Cal-o-Meter
          </a>
          . For pizza, the point of purchase is most often
online or over the phone.
         </p>
         <p>
          In an op-ed published last year in
          <em>
           The Hill
          </em>
          , the CEO of
Dominos, J. Patrick Doyle,
          <a href="http://thehill.com/blogs/congress-blog/politics/176887-on-menu-regulations-government-must-do-better">
           criticized
          </a>
          the proposed rule as a one-size-fits-all set of
rules for menu labeling that will result in wide calorie ranges for
entire pizzas on menus consumers will not even see, but will cost
small business owners thousands of dollars a year.
         </p>
         <p>
          Those costs can range upwards of $5,000 per franchise location.
The cost to grocersa cost that, as with pizza, would no doubt be
passed on to consumers in the form of higher food costswould be
even greater.
         </p>
         <p>
          Why so costly?
         </p>
         <p>
          "With 34 million ways to make a
pizza, it makes no common sense to require this industrywhich
already discloses calories voluntarily, for the most partto
attempt to cram this information on menu boards in small
storefronts, says Lynn Liddle, who chairs the
          <a href="http://www.americanpizzacommunity.com/page.asp?g=pizza&amp;content=about">
           American Pizza Community
          </a>
          , a coalition representing much of the
American pizza industry, in an email to me.
         </p>
         <p>
          To put that number of pizza choices in perspective, consider
that nearly
          <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population">
           every
single person living in Canada today
          </a>
          could order an entirely
different pizza from Dominoswhere the chain is
          <a href="http://www.dominos.ca/">
           also popular
          </a>
          .
         </p>
         <p>
          Oppositionfrom those like me who say the proposed rules go too
far and those who argue they dont go far enoughhas
          <a href="http://www.keepfoodlegal.org/content/fda-rightly-pulls-restaurant-menu-labeling-guidance">
           slowed
          </a>
          the FDA as the agency has sought to craft a final
rule.
         </p>
         <p>
          Some in the latter camp seem certain that others should simply
divine a way to comply with the FDAs proposed rules.
         </p>
         <p>
          I think pizza places should label caloriesreally, they can
figure out how to do it, New York University professor of public
health Marion Nestle
          <a href="http://www.foodpolitics.com/2012/07/pizza-chains-want-easier-or-no-menu-labeling/">
           wrote
          </a>
          at her Food Politics blog over the summer.
         </p>
         <p>
          One way pizza chains might do so is to post a calorie range for
all menu items. But pizza makers argue that such a range would be
too broad and, anyways,
          <a href="http://articles.washingtonpost.com/2012-06-19/business/35460574_1_pizza-chains-force-chain-restaurants-pizza-hut">
           90 percent of their customers
          </a>
          order their food online or over
the phonemaking in-store posting not just costly but also
irrelevant and unhelpful to consumers.
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Didn't I see on Drudge that now they're saying fatties live
longer? I didn't read the actual article but the thrust of what I
imagine in my head it was goes something like this:
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             Dr. Melik: This morning for breakfast he requested
something called "wheat germ, organic honey and tiger's
milk."
             <br/>
             Dr. Aragon: [chuckling] Oh, yes. Those are the charmed substances
that some years ago were thought to contain life-preserving
properties.
             <br/>
             Dr. Melik: You mean there was no deep fat? No steak or cream pies
or... hot fudge?
             <br/>
             Dr. Aragon: Those were thought to be unhealthy... precisely the
opposite of what we now know to be true.
             <br/>
            </blockquote>
            <p>
             I think pizza places should just display for any pizza the
largest calorie count for a pie that they could possibly construct,
rather than try to calculate that actual amounts for a the various
combinations of toppings. If you're eating pizza, you've already
surrendered in the caloric battle.
            </p>
           </div>

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Not bad, PS. Not bad at all. IMMO, there are two things that are
the motherfucking Devil in what passes as nutritional science:
            </p>
            <p>
             1) BMI (absolutely useless as a measure of overall health).
            </p>
            <p>
             2) The USDA Food Pyramid (a weapon of mass destruction).
            </p>
            <p>
             One of the best: Genetics and family HX. I'm a firm believer
that one's genetic profile showing genetic lineage gives a better
idea of what may be the optimal diet for a given person, since most
phenotypes (such as fat, carb, and protein metabolism) are inwardly
expressed. Tests measuring blood chemistries, full lipid panel, and
complete metabolic panels, and cardiac enzyme tests are going to
tell me a lot more about your overall health than the notoriously
unreliable first hand account of dietary intake.
            </p>
            <p>
             Evolutionary factors play a big part into how a given diet
reacts to a specific person, and my go-to population displaying
this is the Native American population in OK WRT Teh Diabeetus and
other endocrine and cardiac maladies. Basically, they adopted a
diet that from an evolutionary POV, were not prepared to tolerate.
Same goes for Americans of Black African ancestry.
            </p>
           </div>

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Afternoon Doc.
            </p>
            <p>
             I recently read
             <em>
              The 10,000 Year Explosion
             </em>
             so I'm more
convinced that evolutionary change can happen quite fast, say 1000
years or ~40 generations for a standard deviation. Also genes
travel around unless they are well isolated, so there seems that ag
adapted genes quickly spread into Europe from the Middle East. Does
that mean a person from an ag culture is optimized for eating wheat
or rice? Prolly not, but better off than someone whose ancestors
were HGs a century ago.
            </p>
            <p>
             But isn't the big thing with DM2 and other such DOCs the more
recent additions like processed sugar and seed oils that no one is
really adapted to eating? I suppose an adaptation to complex carbs
like wheat could help with simple carbs, also.
            </p>
           </div>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Afteroon, PS.
            </p>
            <p>
             <em>
              But isn't the big thing with DM2 and other such DOCs the
more recent additions like processed sugar and seed oils that no
one is really adapted to eating? I suppose an adaptation to complex
carbs like wheat could help with simple carbs, also.
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             Yes, IMMO, that is true, and why I have a lower opinion of a
diet that relies on highly processed and enriched, nutrient and
caloric dense foods combined with a sedentary lifestyle. Not to say
they're evil, but if you're a layabout lardass who eats nothing but
Lil' Debbie snack cakes and in large amounts like two boxes a day,
then what do you expect? Seed oils are not as well researched, but
I think that various sources of cooking oil is better than relying
on exclusively any specific one. As you know, Euro-landia has
declared war on trans-fats.
            </p>
            <p>
             The wheat thing is developed over time, but I am not quite as
convinced of the 1000 years. UKR depends on wheat as a staple, and
overall, are pretty tolerant of it as DM2 rates are lower than the
USA. Lack of iodine in the diet, rickets, and other nutritional
deficits are more of a concern.
            </p>
           </div>
           <!--Comment actions (Reply/Login/Register)-->

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             <em>
              I heard stories of some kids in Oakland CA getting rickets
due to the lack of sunlight in the projects.
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             That's actually true, Tulpa. Of the darker pigmented children,
and this is true of dark skinned people, are more at risk of
developing rickets D/T lack of calcium and phosphorus in the diet,
and since melanin absorbs sunlight, the UV light doesn't stimulate
Vit D production.
            </p>
            <p>
             This is why you see rickets in African countries where both
widespread poverty and malnutrition exist. In UKR, it's the
poverty, though there are bona fide Afro-Ukrainians living
here.
            </p>
           </div>

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             I suppose that's true, but the down side is fair skinned people
are at a demonstrably higher risk of developing skin cancers, such
as the melanoma and the dreaded basal cell carcinoma, so it's a
trade off.
            </p>
            <p>
             The discolouration is another marked one, as it is harder to
identify those conditions and diseases, even simple ones like
contusions, for that very reason. The up side is darker skinned
people produce a lot more collagen and the skin is tougher, so much
more resistant to the effects of aging, and to a certain degree,
dehydration.
            </p>
           </div>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Sadly, I'm afraid that the most reliable way to keep track of
your calories is to see whether you feel hungry all the time. If
you don't, you're in positive caloric balance. I don't think it
matters how many kcal you take in at any given meal, day, or week
-- in the long run the only way for most of us to not gain weight,
let alone lose it, is to feel unsatisfied, and that's regardless of
composition, Pound Head.
            </p>
           </div>

           <div class="content">
            <blockquote>
             In fact, writes Baylen Linnekin, complying with the
proposed menu-labeling rule would be somewhere between costly and
impossible for tens of thousands of pizza and grocery
chains.
            </blockquote>
            <p>
             Like the feds give a shit.
            </p>
            <p>
             The intent of the law is to ensure that there are going to be
places that fail to meet certain provisions. If they couldn't
fashion the rules so that some businesses would fail, they wouldn't
hold any power. And what's the point of taking a job as a regulator
if you can't flaunt your power every once in a while.
            </p>
            <p>
             It's designed to be impossible for some, and they'll make sure
that they fashion the rules so that they're impossible for whoever
they want it to be impossible for.
            </p>

at the same time also makes centralized planning easier (yes, I
know we dislike it but it is there).
            </p>
           </div>
           <!--Comment actions (Reply/Login/Register)-->

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             You just described why, in most places, mass public transit
cannot work. Both models are inefficient; in neither are there
enough passengers per mile. And even in places like Boston or New
York where public transit does work in terms of numbers who ride,
it's subsidized by gas taxes out of the fucking wazoo (
             <a href="http://madlibertarianguy.net/2012/02/13/flyover-country" rel="nofollow">
              around
80% of the cost of public transit is subsidized
             </a>
             ). Take away
those subsidies, and the ridership numbers would drop like a
rock.
            </p>
           </div>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             There should be no subsidization or regulation of any
transportation or land use. Local roads should be maintained by
adjacent property owners and other people who use them, or allowed
to revert to dirt. This is exactly how roads started out in the
United States, and worked fine until people started to demand that
roads be made convenient for cars (actually bicycles before that)
at taxpayer expense.
            </p>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             I think that "sprawl" is subsidized or not fully market based
due to zoning and land use policy, as well as centralized planning
and public funding of infrastructure and utilities- especially
roads. Roads should have never been "improved" with taxpayer money
to make them convenient for car travel. They should have been left
as common areas owned by adjacent property owners and unrestricted
to use for any form of travel. This would have resulted in
lifestyle situations much closer to what environmentalist and
"urbanist" types claim to want, with private transportation such as
railroads being much more economical.
            </p>
           </div>

             <br/>
            </p>
            <blockquote>
             I don't have a problem with subsidies per se, just bad
ones that tend to result from cronyism rather than social
concern.
            </blockquote>
            <p>
             <br/>
             What difference would it make either way, Tony? Subsidies are still
forced transfers of property. Subsidies are NEVER Pareto efficient
and create market distortions that lead to bad price information
and malinvestment. Intentions are irrelevant.
            </p>

             But I also don't believe that you're any less
interested in social engineering than I am.
            </blockquote>
            <p>
             <br/>
             In my case, I'm not interested in social engineering of any kind.
Only individuals get to decide how to build their own lives, not a
self-appointed improver.
            </p>

            <p>
             Obviously this law is a terrible idea, but methinks Linnekin is
exaggerating the compliance difficulties to bolster his position.
Pizza places could just list the calorie totals for base
configurations and then the calorie totals for each topping
separately. Mushrooms on a 14" sausage pizza has the same amount of
calories as mushrooms on a 14" pepperoni pizza, so you don't need
separate listings for the combos.
            </p>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Best parts:
            </p>
            <p>
             Alan Howard, a Los Angeles dentist who says he has only once
been able to find a charging spot since he bought a Nissan Leaf
last year, says making electric drivers pay for parking isn't the
answer.
            </p>
            <p>
             "I love it freebut they need to have some kind of system," he
says. "Maybe a valet who can move the cars around."
            </p>
            <p>
             Jack Sheng, an e-commerce-company owner who bought a Nissan Leaf
last year, says electric vehicles should be able to park free in
all eight lots, not just the two with charging stations.
            </p>
            <p>
             "If they're trying to get people to drive green cars and reduce
the pollution, shouldn't the policy be applicable to the whole
terminal?" says Mr. Sheng, miffed that he had to pay to park
recently while picking up a friend from an international
flight.
            </p>

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <blockquote>
             Nancy Huehnergarth, executive director of the New York
State Healthy Eating and Physical Activity Alliance, tells me she
supports the proposed FDA rule because it would provide information
at the right place and time.
             <br/>
             <br/>
             "The key [???] to menu labeling and getting it to work [?????] is
that people see the calories at the point of purchase," says
Huehnergarth.
            </blockquote>
            <p>
             <br/>
             She's saying that as if customers gave a rat's hairy crack about
the caloric content of a fucking
             <em>
              pizza
             </em>
             that they're about
to pick up at the counter.
            </p>
            <p>
             It is clear that goodist busy-bodies see the world backwards:
They think people
             <em>
              should
             </em>
             want something and then strive to
get it to them without even asking. In the real world, things are
             <em>
              offered
             </em>
             voluntarily and,
             <em>
              if
             </em>
             people like it, it is
then discovered for others to follow up. A market is a process of
discovery. For bureaucrats and other meddlers, that's just too
messy a process. Why wait for people to make their minds if you can
impose it by force?
            </p>
           </div>

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             the idea that it's propaganda is why i don't bother engaging
with trolls.
            </p>
            <p>
             what i support, and what i readily admit is not consistent with
"pure" libertarianism, is simply a label describing what's in X, in
the case of food, simple ingredient and nutritional information.
this limits nobody's choice; it simply arms consumers with
information. information, not propaganda. reporting how many
kilocalories, grams of protein, etc. is in a product is not
"propaganda". it's information. it has a bias towards no
institution or ideology. it's information. i will continue to
oppose transfat bans, happy meal bans, or any ban of a food item
(raw milk, etc.) apart from something obvious like selling
strichnine as food :)
            </p>
            <p>
             but arming consumers with info imo is a strong enough good to
override the principles that say we should let the market operate
unfettered. again, i admit it - inconsistent with pure
libertarianism, but must be paid for by govt. imo and again, limits
nobody's choice or freedoms, just increases access to data.
            </p>
           </div>

isnt allowed.

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             <em>
              Serious question, sloop: Do cigarettes cause
cancer?
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             False analogy IMO, because the cigarette companies themselves
agreed to post the government warnings on cigarette packaging and
the displays the owners of stores are not required to post. IIRC,
the government tried to put graphic warnings on the packaging the
companies didn't agree to, and
             <a href="http://www.crainsnewyork.com/article/20121205/ADVERTISING_MARKETING/121209950" rel="nofollow">
              the federal courts smacked them down
             </a>
             .
            </p>
            <p>
             What's the difference here? You're forcing someone to advertise
something they do not voluntarily wish to advertise. That's
propaganda to me, regardless of whether they're forced to pay for
it or if they're forced to let somebody else do it, especially when
the information is readily available on the internet (to any
consumer that wishes to look them up). For example,
             <a href="http://caloriecount.about.com/calories-mozzarella-whole-milk-cheese-i1026" rel="nofollow">
              here's the nutritional information on mozzarella cheese
             </a>
             .
            </p>

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             <em>
              False analogy IMO, because the cigarette companies
themselves agreed to post the government warnings on cigarette
packaging and the displays the owners of stores are not required to
post.
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             Only after:
            </p>
            <p>
             A) The court required them to do so, after demonstrable proof
was shown they cause all the things listed on the package. (Second
hand smoke is largely bullshit, IMMO. Though you should never smoke
around a developing baby or toddler.)
            </p>
            <p>
             B) Current FDA law cannot prohibit the manufacture,
distribution, and sale of cigarettes.
            </p>
            <p>
             <em>
              You're forcing someone to advertise something they do not
voluntarily wish to advertise.
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             Would you voluntarily take any drug without knowing first all
the possible side effects? Also, companies pay people to post on
the Internet relevant information WRT their products, foreseeable
to mitigate risk against lawsuits.
            </p>

found that they violate the 1A. The companies continue to post the
text warnings voluntarily as they have done since 1966.
            </p>

             And no, I wouldn't voluntarily take any drug without knowing the
possible side effects. But that has more to do with me not being an
idiot that puts anything in my body without knowing how it impacts
it, and less to do with the government compelling a company to do
so. I guess these laws are so people can be lazy about their health
and can leave it to somebody else to be responsible for their
decision-making ability. Well, when that butts up against the 1A, I
have a problem with that.

           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Actually, you were not mistaken. Cigarette warning labels have
been mandatory since 1966. The Wikipedia page you cited is about
warning labels, not just the ones proposed in 2009. The line you
quoted cites a source with the same sentence you quoted, only
expanded. Here it is:
            </p>
            <p>
             "The United States was the first nation to require a health
warning on cigarette packages 45 years ago."
            </p>
            <p>
             <a href="http://www.nytimes.com/2011/06/22/health/policy/22smoke.html?_r=0" rel="nofollow">
              http://www.nytimes.com/2011/06......html?_r=0
             </a>
            </p>
            <p>
             The article was written in 2011, so 45 years ago is 1966.
            </p>
            <p>
             Sloopy's link says this:
            </p>
            <p>
             "1966-01-01: Health warnings on Cigarette Packs begin
            </p>
            <p>
             In order to adhere to the recently passed Cigarette Labeling and
Advertising Act, cigarette packages begin to carry labels which
read: "Caution--cigarette smoking may be hazardous to your
health."
            </p>
            <p>
             <a href="http://archive.tobacco.org/resources/history/Tobacco_Historynotes.html#aa10" rel="nofollow">
              http://archive.tobacco.org/res......html#aa10
             </a>
            </p>
            <p>
             If you have to adhere to a law, that means it is mandatory, not
voluntary.
            </p>
           </div>

Thirty. While it's true that US forces killed OBL this is still

             No disagreements there, RBS. However, if I decide to see the
movie, it probably won't give me cancer or Teh Diabeetus (or maybe
it will.-)
            </p>
            <p>
             I jest, but if there is something that could cause demonstrable
harm, I would like to know so I can make an informed decision. If I
was the proprietor, I would provide the info so I don't get sued
(and in a Free Society, this is all that should be required).
            </p>
            <p>
             People still do both intelligent and stupid things with informed
consent about risk, stuff goes wrong or not to their liking, and
they get sue happy quickly, looking for someone else besides
themselves to blame. I know this firsthand, unfortunately.

            <p>
             Doc groovus--
            </p>
            <p>
             <em>
              Do cigarettes cause cancer?
             </em>
            </p>
            <p>
             Almost surely... but, I don't
             <strong>
              know
             </strong>
             .
            </p>
            <p>
             But I do
             <strong>
              know
             </strong>
             that the euphemism "coffin nails"
dates from before 1860.
            </p>
            <p>
             I do
             <strong>
              know
             </strong>
             that "The Three Stooges" often showed
cigarette packs with a 'skull and crossbones' on the face.
            </p>
            <p>
             I do
             <strong>
              know
             </strong>
             that Heinlein repeatedly mentions the
downsides of tobacco in his juvenile novels.
            </p>
            <p>
             I do
             <strong>
              know
             </strong>
             that even Walter Raleigh wrote about
the negatives to smoking.
            </p>
            <p>
             I smoke 12-15 cigarettes a day. I do this knowing my paternal
grandmother died from lung cancer(smoker).
            </p>
            <p>
             Meanwhile, my dad died of pancreatic cancer at 42- 8 yrs after
             <em>
              quitting
             </em>
             cigarettes.
            </p>
            <p>
             My Aunt Ethel is still kicking at 80+, despite smoking for 50
yrs (She quit 8 yrs ago due to COPD). She was in such bad shape
three yrs ago to need an oxygen tank- now she is off the oxy, and
seems healthy.
            </p>
            <p>
             Mom smoked for over 45 yrs- she's still kicking at 73 after two
heart attacks (she didn't quit smoking until after the first).
            </p>
            <p>
             My sister smoked from age 14. She died at age 45-- from liver
failure due to alcoholism.
            </p>
            <p>
             Smoking kills- if something else
             <em>
              doesn't
             </em>
             kill you
first.
            </p>
           </div>

          <!--Comment Head-->
          <li class="com-block reply0" data-comment-id="comment_3467710" data-comment-timestamp="1357417659" data-user-id="455" id="comment_3467710">
           <p class="meta">
            <strong>
             <a href="http://users.bestweb.net/~robgood" rel="nofollow">
              Robert
             </a>
            </strong>
            <span class="pipe">
             |
            </span>
            <strong>
             <time datetime="2013-01-05T21:27:39+00:00">
              1.5.13 @ 4:27PM
             </time>
            </strong>
            <span class="pipe">
             |
            </span>
            <a class="permalink" href="#comment_3467710">
             #
            </a>
           </p>
           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             This has been a fall-back position for libertarians for a long
time when there were threats to ban stuff. "Don't ban cigarets,
label them hazardous and let consumers decide." "Don't ban campaign
donations, just make them be disclosed." But there's no broad
threat to ban food per se, just a few around the edges, so this is
a compromise with a threat that doesn't exist.
            </p>
            <p>
             The silly thing is that even without disclosures by the
purveyors of foods themselves, there have long been tables of the
nutritional values of foods in general. People have a pretty good
idea of what pizza with sausage is generally, so there are standard
calorie, sodium, etc. counts. The difference in those terms from
the products of one establishment to those of another have got to
be minuscule.
            </p>
            <p>
             I should get a look at the enabling legislation for this, which
has pretty much snuck by me. The Federal Food, Drug and Cosmetic
Act over the years has remained remarkably confined to regulating
only actual interstate commerce, with few exceptions, rather than
local businesses. This would be a major deviation from that
path.
            </p>
           </div>
           <!--Comment actions (Reply/Login/Register)-->

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Oh, come on. Even you cosmotarians must know that complying with
government regulations is just as simple as printing out a label
and slapping it on the counter.
            </p>
            <p>
             It's not like the state would issue arbitrary and absurd rules
that would harm small businesses disproportionately, and that the
consequences of these rules would fly almost entirely under the
public's radar. It's not like even one single chicken farmer has
ever spent an afternoon arguing with state poultry inspectors over
whether a calcium deposit (aka a "bump") on an egg constituted a
violation of state health laws. It's not like federal regulation of
menus would ever result in increased expenses, such as the ones
that BL outlines in his article, or even abuses. I challenge you to
name even one business that was destroyed due to state intervention
into the economy. Excluding Nice Cream. And Polar Pure. And pretty
much every Ma-and-Pa gas station in the country.
            </p>
            <p>
             You glibertarians need to stop resisting and realize that the
state is only here to help.
            </p>

            <p>
             True Threats to Pizza:
            </p>
            <p>
             1. Deep dish. It might be good (and Lou Malnati's is good), but
it ain't pizza.
             <br/>
             2. Stuffed crusts- Jesus, what a bad fucking idea.
             <br/>
             3. Pineapple- You might like it, but it ain't pizza. And liking it
says something about you, and it's not a positive thing.
             <br/>
             4. Sugar- What the fuck is THAT all about? Sugar in the crust,
sugar in the sauce. What. The. Fuck????
             <br/>
             5. Barbeque sauce- I shit you not, when we lived in Austin, one of
the popular chains there added this to the tomato sauce. We took
one bite, spat it out, then put the rest of the "pizza" into our
dog's food dish. She ran over, took a sniff, and walked away
without touching it. Our dog will eat her own vomit but wouldn't
touch a Gatti's pizza.

           <div class="content">
            <p>
             6. Ranch sauce- Whenever I want to defend the middle of the
country against the arrogance and snobbery of the coasts, the
concept of dipping pizza in ranch sauce comes up. At the thought of
that, so does my lunch. OK, maybe the middle of the country IS a
wasteland.
             <br/>
             7. Tabasco- who came up with THAT idea? This makes me rethink my
opposition to capital punishment.
             <br/>
             8. More than two toppings- Want a soggy mess? Go to it. Want a
bland base with junk piled on top? You're welcome to try. It ain't
pizza.
             <br/>
             9. California Pizza Kitchen- "California" and "Pizza" are two words
that don't belong together, and this is proof. You pay a premium
for some horrible construct (it ain't pizza) which has all flavor
scientifically removed.
            </p>
            <p>
             Don't diss Chicago- it is the home to Spacca Napoli, one of the
two or three best pizzas in the US. And I mean REAL pizza, Tipo
Fino 00 flour, San Marzano tomatoes, hand-made mozzarella,
wood-burning oven.
            </p>

           </p>
           <!--Comment Body-->
           <div class="content">
            <p>
             Well, as an Italian myself, I've had my share of conversations
about what constitutes a great pizza and sauce with many a mother
in both the old country and here.
            </p>
            <p>
             Particularly about the sugar. EDG, yes to some, sugar balances
out the acid. But as I've learned over the years and speaking to
chefs, you don't need sugar if you use a great tomato sauce like
San Marzano or Reggina.
            </p>
            <p>
             Buy D.O.P straight from Italy and don't use sugar again. Try
it.
            </p>
            <p>
             What I do use from time to time for kicks is a dash of Balsamic
vinegar in the sauce but that's for my own twisted pleasure.
            </p>
           </div>

Nations.?Markets don't work without transparency or accountability.

don't make pizza. Someone else will. Its a market.
            </p>

      <script>
       $(document).ready(function() {
    	//no point in binding to swipe events if there's no touch support in the browser (and it goofs up selection)
		if ('ontouchstart' in window) {
			//in an effort to reduce false horizontal swipes from bold vertical flicks on larger screens...
			swipeThreshold = 200;
			$("#content-col").swipe( {
				threshold: swipeThreshold,
				allowPageScroll: 'vertical',
			    swipeLeft:function(event, direction, distance, duration, fingerCount) {
			    	//we're going to put in a non-zero fingercount check here to exclude mouse drags
					if (fingerCount &amp;&amp; $('#nextpage').length &amp;&amp; $('#nextpage').attr("href").length) {
					    window.location.href=  $('#nextpage').attr("href");
					}
			    },
			    swipeRight:function(event, direction, distance, duration, fingerCount) {
			    	//we're going to put in a non-zero fingercount check here to exclude mouse drags
					if (fingerCount &amp;&amp; $('#previouspage').length &amp;&amp; $('#previouspage').attr("href").length) {
				    	window.location.href = $('#previouspage').attr("href");
					}
			    }
			});
		}
    });
      </script>

         $(function(){
	$("#topstoriessidebar .selector a").click(function(e){
		var $this=$(this), catref = $this.attr("data-category"),
			$sidebar = $this.parent().parent().parent();
		e.preventDefault();
		$sidebar.find('&gt; .selector &gt; li').removeClass('active');
		$this.parent().addClass('active');
		$sidebar.find('&gt; .inner &gt; .stories').hide().filter('[data-category='+catref+']').show();
	});
});
